Figure 4.18 displays < CT > and < CP > for the six cases, and CT and CP can be
found in Table 4.1. Here clearly can see that both CT and CP vary periodically according
to the surging frequency !S of their case, and their amplitudes are related to V, which is
related to both AS & !S, where larger V brings larger varying amplitudes. However, there
are upper limits for both CT and CP, and they are related to stalling, which will be more
elaborated in subsection 4.5.3. Moreover, if focusing around the values of < CT > for the
’¼üxed case, one can ’¼ünd out that the extents of under-shoots are more signi’¼ücant compare to
the over-shoots for the surging cases, and the di’¼Ćerences are become larger with larger V.
This is clearly documented with < CT >+ and < CT >ŌłÆin Table 4.1, and it is also re’¼éected
with CT, from which cases with bigger V have smaller CT for the considered surging settings
here. As for CP, seems that surging cases with lower V (V = 0.11 or 0.22) have slightly
bigger values compare to the ’¼üxed case, while the opposite happen for the cases with bigger
V (V = 0.44). It is suggested that the velocity triangle and stalling can be used to explain
these behaviours of CT & CP. More detail discussions about stalling and the causes of lower
CT for surging rotor can be found in subsection 4.5.3.
Further observing the curves of < CT > and < CP >, one can ’¼ünd that they are almost
in-phase with the surging motions, and this agrees with most of the ’¼ündings in literature
[10, 21, 29]. It can be seen that curves of < CT > and < CP > cross the values of ’¼üxed case
at around < t > /TŌī”= 0.5 and 1.5, which are the timing when surging velocity of rotor is
0.0 m/s (ŽåS = 0.5ŌćĪor 1.5ŌćĪ, V0,app = V0). However, slight hysteric e’¼Ćects still appear after
plotting < CT > against surging velocity of rotor VWT in Figure 4.19. Notice that VWT re’¼éects
the phase of surging (Equation 2.26). While the hollow marks in Figure 4.19 represent the
minimums and maximums of < CT > a case should get according to quasi-steady solutions
form the auxiliary simulation cases in Table 4.2 together with their values.
Notably in

4.3. Laminar In’¼éow Conditions with Di’¼Ćerent AS and !S
4.3.2
Velocity and TKE Fields
The ’¼üelds of < u >0ŌćĪfor the six cases of single NREL 5MW rotor with di’¼Ćerent surging
amplitudes AS and surging frequencies !S under laminar in’¼éow conditions are displayed
in Figure 4.20 (cases 1 & 5-9). Instantaneous contours of u are not displayed since they
are very similar to < u >0ŌćĪ’¼üelds due to the very low values <TKE>0ŌćĪ’¼üelds which will
be displayed later. Clearly that except for the ’¼üxed case, all the other ’¼üve show periodic
structures in their wakes, which are the periodic low speed bubbles (PLSB) introduced in
section 4.2. Note it can be seen that the repeating frequencies of PLSB are exactly the
values of the casesŌĆÖ !S; and even with the di’¼Ćerent frequencies, the PLSB basically shares
same characteristics, having slower regions in the wakes but faster at just outside the wakes.
Also, one should be aware of the 3D nature of the wakes and actually PLSB are more like
rings. More detail descriptions and analysis can be found in subsection 4.5.1 & 4.5.2, where
PLSB will be more explored.
The ’¼üelds <TKE>0ŌćĪare shown in Figure 4.21. Once again, values for <TKE>0ŌćĪ’¼üelds
are very low, even at the vicinity of the rotors. As being stated in the previous sections, this
indicates the ’¼éow is highly repeatable and deterministic, and this seems to be the theme
for the laminar in’¼éow cases with the simulation framework employed in this thesis. And
very interesting phenomenon is that with bigger AS, the ’¼üelds of <TKE>0ŌćĪseems to be
smaller (Figure 4.21), and the ’¼üxed case has the most pronounced values. Fang et al. [60]
had reported similar phenomena using IDDES with geometric resolved rotor, stating that
tip-vorticity related structures actually breakup earlier while the rotor is ’¼üxed compare to
a surging one. The above phenomenon implies that with bigger AS, the ’¼éow ’¼üelds actually
become more repeatable, or in other words, more predictable. And this may be the reason
why for x/D Ōēź6, recovery rate of uDisk for case AS = 2 m are greater than cases with larger
AS (same !S), since the ’¼éow structures are more chaotic with smaller AS. As for varying
!S, the ’¼éow ’¼üelds are still seemed to be more repeatable compared to the ’¼üxed case with
the three considered !S. While the trend with !S is not as clear as AS, since they have quite
di’¼Ćerent patterns of <TKE>0ŌćĪat the downstream regions.
Fields of TKE for the three surging cases under laminar in’¼éow conditions are very
similar, and thus they are not displayed. One can ’¼ünd the TKE contour for surging case
with AS = 4 m & !S = 0.63 rad/s under laminar in’¼éow conditions in case 6 of Figure 4.9.

The ’¼üeld of Ōłåp for the eight cases are displayed in Figure 4.22. In these contours, two
strips of lower pressure regions appear in the ’¼üve surging cases. Moreover, an important
’¼ünding is that cases with smaller V have more noticeable high and low pressure regions in
front and after the neutral positions of the rotor, and the ’¼üxed cases having the biggest
pressure jump. And for the case with V = 0.44 (AS = 8 m & !S = 0.63 rad/s and AS = 4 m
& !S = 1.27 rad/s), their pressure bubbles are signi’¼ücantly smaller compared to the other
cases, which should be related to the curves in Figure 4.18a. This is reasonable together
with the ’¼ündings that CT drops as V becomes bigger in Table 4.1. More detail discussions
are in subsection 4.5.3. As for ’¼üelds of Ōłåp and < Ōłåp >0ŌćĪ, since they are similar with the

laminar cases in last section (section 4.2), they are not displayed.

Contours of !y ’¼üelds are not displayed

and 1.27 rad/s, it is obvious that periods of the vortical structure are exactly the same with
!S, since these vortical structures are results of the merges of the tip vorticites released
within a completed surging cycle. However, for !S = 0.32 rad/s, during the formation of
the vortical structures, tip vorticies within a complete surging cycle did not form together
as one vortex structure, but rather two, with one stronger (eg. the one at x/D between

result of di’¼Ćerent varying rates for the inter-distances between the tip vorticies. Moreover,
for the cases considered here, the ones with higher !S would form their vortical structures
at more upstream positions. Furthermore, for the three cases with !S = 0.63 rad/s but
with di’¼Ćerent AS, magnitudes of their vortical structures are similar; this is mainly due to
that these vortical structures are results of the merges of the tip vorticites released within a
completed surging cycle, together with the rotational speed being kept unchanged during the
simulations and the fact that both CT and CP for the three cases are similar (implying the
release tip vorticies have similar magnitudes). See subsection 4.5.1 for more details about
the merging process of the vortical structures.

4.5. Further Discussions
4.5
Further Discussions
4.5.1
Interactions of Tip Vorticies Within a Surging Cycle
As displayed in the previous sections in this chapter, surging cases will result in distinct
vortical structures in their wakes, especially for for the cases with laminar in’¼éow conditions.
As been shown in Figure 4.23, the modes of the vortical structures are mainly dominated by
surging frequencies !S, while surging amplitudes AS have less in’¼éuences for the considered
cases. Figure 4.31 to 4.33 depict how the vortical structures are generated and convected
with series of sequential snapshots for cases with !S being 0.32, 0.63, and 1.27 rad/s (cases
in section 4.3). In the three sets of plots, the boxes of red dashed-line indicate the vortical
structures formed within a surging cycle, and it can be seen that the vortical structures for
the three cases are formed through merging tip-vorticies through rolling up. The merging
processes are triggered since the inter-distances between tip-vorticies are varied during a
surging cycle, which brings up the imbalance of inductance forces among them. The dif-
ferences of the inter-distances between tip-vorticies are the most pronounced as comparing
ŽåS = 0.5ŌćĪwith ŽåS = 1.5ŌćĪin Figure 4.31 to 4.33 (focus on the regions just after the rotors),
especially for the cases with higher !S. It is worth noting again that each pair of the vortical
structures appeared in the ’¼ügures are actually a ring, and for cases of !S = 0.63 rad/s and
!S = 1.27 rad/s, each pair of the vortical structures corresponds to a complete surging cycle;
as for the case !S = 0.32 rad/s, a completed surge cycle corresponds to two pairs (rings) of
vortical structures, with a pair stronger and another weaker.

4.5. Further Discussions
4.5.2
Detail Discussions about of PLSB
This section discusses about the periodic low speed bubbles (PLSB) presented in the
previous sections. Other than the y = 0 planes, several selected cases and quantities are also
analyzed with yz-planes at x/D being 3, 5, and 8. Note that for the laminar cases, since that
the values of <TKE>ŽåS ’¼üelds are generally very low, instantaneous ’¼üelds and phase-locked
averaged ’¼üelds are treated interchangeably in this section, as long as the considered ŽåS is
same.
A brief analysis with proper orthogonal decomposition (POD) using the phase-locking
’¼üelds of y-component vorticity !y,0ŌćĪwas conducted in Appendix D; cases with ’¼üxed and
surging rotor under both laminar and turbulent in’¼éow conditions are considered. However,
not much additional information could be obtained besides the phase-locked averaged ’¼üelds.
Thus, simply displaying phase-locked averaged ’¼üelds may be more suitable for this thesis
project for the sake of brevity.
General Structures of PLSB
Figure 4.34 shows the corresponding u ’¼üelds in the y = 0 plane with di’¼Ćerent ŽåS, and
Figure 4.35 to 4.38 presents the phase-locked averaged streamwise velocity based on di’¼Ćerent
ŽåS (< u >ŽåS) on the yz-planes. The surging setting for the case being considered here are
AS = 4 m and !S = 0.63 rad/s (case 6). Note that the locations of red dashed-line boxes
in Figure 4.34 are identical with the ones in Figure 4.32 (!y ’¼üelds), and a pair (ring) of
PLSB are enclosed in every boxes. Also, it is now very clear that PLSB is actually ring
with the plots of < u >ŽåS on yz-planes; however, instead of being actual axis-symmetric, the
structures of PLSB is only symmetry about every 120ŌŚ”(P3). Notice that ’¼üelds of yz-planes
with di’¼Ćerent ŽåS depicts the convection of PLSB.
Figure 4.39 is the phase-locked averaged velocity ’¼üelds for ŽåS = 0ŌćĪ(< u >0ŌćĪ) of the
case AS = 4 m & !S = 1.27 rad/s (case 9) on the yz-planes. In general, they share similar
features with the case AS = 4 m & !S = 0.63 rad/s presented earlier.

4.5. Further Discussions
Highly Complex but Highly Deterministic
Figure 4.40 and 4.41 displayed the ’¼üelds of < !x >0ŌćĪfor the cases !S = 0.63 rad/s &
AS = 4 m and !S = 1.27 rad/s & AS = 4 m (cases 6 and 9). Together with the < u >0ŌćĪ
’¼üelds shown before, it can be said that the wake systems are highly complex. However,
with the <TKE>0ŌćĪ’¼üelds (Figure 4.42, 4.43, & 4.29) for the two cases, the very low values
indicate that the wakes are repeatable and highly deterministic, lacking randomness. And
this is the reason which led to the pro’¼üles of u for laminar surging cases (eg. Figure 4.17)
are not in Gaussian shapes as well. Also that highly repeatable process makes structures
with highly localized characteristics (eg. vortical structures in Figure 4.32) pass through the
exact same locations for every surging cycles, resulting in the kinks in the u pro’¼üles for the
laminar cases with surging rotor.
Slight asymmetries about z/D = 0 of the u ’¼üelds can be observed, especially for the
case of !S = 1.27 rad/s (case 9, see Figure E.7). These asymmetries can be explained by
the 120ŌŚ”symmetries displayed before. Figure 4.44 and 4.45 display the u ’¼üelds on yz-planes
for the two cases (!S = 0.63 & 1.27 rad/s) with laminar in’¼éow conditions, and clearly that
120ŌŚ”symmetries still occurs with the time-averaged quanties. The 120ŌŚ”symmetries become
less obvious as traveling to more downstream positions. However, full axis-symmetric is not
arrived. This may relate to the decay of the coherent structures and numeric errors of using
hexahedral grids to simulate semi-axis-symmetric system. However, further investigation
about this is not conducted in this thesis.
Figure 4.46 to 4.48 display the ’¼üelds of < u >0ŌćĪ, < !x >0ŌćĪ, and u for the case AS = 4 m
and !S = 0.63 rad/s under turbulent in’¼éow conditions (TI 5.3%). It is clear that the wake
structures are much more axis-symmetric as compare to the cases with laminar in’¼éows. This
indicates the randomness introduced by the ambient turbulence homogenizes the wakes in
azimuthal direction.

4.5.3
Expected and Actual Behaviours of < CT > & < CP >
This section discusses the expected and actual behaviour of < CT > & < CP > under
di’¼Ćerent surging conditions; their time averaged values, asymmetries about the values of the
’¼üxed case, and stalling will be analyzed.
In section 4.3 to 4.4, it is shown that the curves of < CT > and < CP > seem to
be very similar with simple harmonic functions (for the cases without signi’¼ücant stalling),
their mechanisms behind are actually rather complicated and hard to come up with simple
analytical solutions. To understand the behaviours of < CT > and < CP > for the full
surging cycle, one should consider all the parameters that appeared in Equation 2.10 and
2.18, including surging velocity VWT, apparent normal velocity seen by the surging rotorŌĆÖs
airfoil sections (actuator line points) Vn,app (Equation 2.17), Vrel, Žå, ŌåĄ, and airfoil polar data
for Cl & Cd.
Expected Behaviours of < CT > & < CP >
To begin with the analysis, instantaneous thrust and power conversion rates (ŌćĀCT & ŌćĀCP )
are introduced. They are linked to the instantaneous rotor thrust & power (T & P) through
the apparent in’¼éow wind speed seen by the rotor V0,app (Equation 2.27), as in Equation 4.1.
Note that unlike ŌćĀCT & ŌćĀCP , CT & CP (CT & CP) are linked to T & P (T & P) through V0,
which is the free stream velocity without considering surging e’¼Ćects.
T = ŌćĀCT V 2

(4.1)

negligible throughout the surging cycle, both the values for time-averaged thrust and power
coe’¼ācients (CT & CP) for the surging cases should be greater than the ’¼üxed case, due to the
inequalities shown in Equation 4.2 and 4.3 (Johlas et al. [11] had done a similar analysis).

And with these facts, values of CT and CP should be bigger for cases with larger V. However,

signi’¼ücantly as V becomes larger. This suggest that ŌćĀCT and ŌćĀCP are not constants during
the surging cycle, and how fn & fŌ£ōare obtained for every actuator line points should be

Actual Behaviours of < CT > & < CP >
If looking closer to the the curves of < CT > and < CP > for the surging cases, for
example in Figure 4.18, one can ’¼ünd that as VWT = 0 m/s, both < CT > and < CP > have
values which are very close of the ’¼üxed case (neutral values), suggesting hysteresis (dynamic
in’¼éow) e’¼Ćects are weak (see Figure 4.19 for more details about hysteresis e’¼Ćects). Moreover,
even though the curves looks quite like sinusoidal, their ’¼éuctuations are not symmetry about
the value for ’¼üxed rotor, especially for the < CT >. Clearly that with the neutral values as
the base points, the under-shoots of < CT > are larger than the over-shoots, and this also
re’¼éects in lower values of CT (Table 4.1). As for CP, its values for ’¼üxed and surging cases
considered in this chapter are similar (slightly bigger for cases with V = 0.11 & 0.22 but
smaller for cases with V = 0.44).
The di’¼Ćerent behaviours of CT and CP as comparing to the neutral values could be
explained by the in’¼éow angle Žå and angle of attack ŌåĄare varying during a surging cycle
simultaneously. As can be seen in Equation 2.18, larger apparent normal velocity seen by
the surging rotor Vn,app (Equation 2.17) will result in a larger Žå and thus lager ŌåĄ, and higher
ŌåĄwill lead to larger lift force L in general (if stalling does not occur). Moreover, usually
bigger L will end up in larger thrust and larger power, since they both directly related
to L (Equation 2.13). However, Žå is also changing as Vn,app varies, and note that L can
be decomposed into ╦ån & ╦åŌ£ō(Equation 4.4), where they are the normal (thrust) component
and tangential component. With basic trigonometry, bigger Žå will lead to smaller cos Žå but
bigger sin Žå, projecting L less to the thrust (normal) component but more to the tangential
component. And thus, risings of CT are less than CP while Vn,app > Vn. While as Vn,app < Vn,
opposite situations occurs, CT drops less while CP drops more.
However, according to
Equation 4.2 and 4.3, it is reasonable that periods of Vn,app > Vn are more important when
it comes to the time-averaged values of CT and CP due to the weighting of V 2
0,app. And thus
Page 91 of 244
==PAGE:123==
4.5. Further Discussions
the results of CT for surging cases being smaller than the ’¼üxed cases while CP remain for
’¼üxed and surging cases are reasonable.
L = |L| cos Žå ╦ån + |L| sin Žå ╦åŌ£ō
(4.4)
4.5.4
Stalling and < CT > & < CP >
The dips in < CT > and < CP > curves for cases with higher V (Figure 4.18) can be
explained with stalling. As the surging velocity VWT becomes large enough to make the angle
of attack ŌåĄof a blade section in Equation 2.12 to exceed the stalling angle ŌåĄstall through
the enlarged in’¼éow angle Žå in Equation 2.18, that blade section will experience stalling and
making Cl to drop while Cd to rise, causing both CT and CP dropping. Note that since ALM
is implemented here, stalling behaviours are modelled through the input polar data; only
the values of Cl and Cd are concerned, and no additional e’¼Ćects such as extra turbulence
from the boundary layer development nor leading edge vortex generated due to stalling are
modelled. Also one should note that polar data used in this thesis are static polar data and
no dynamic-stall model was applied.
Figure 4.49 presents the stalling angle of attack ŌåĄstall for NREL 5MW baseline turbine
along its blade span, and ŌåĄstall is de’¼üned at the ŌåĄwhich it gives the (’¼ürst local) maximum
Cl. Note that since NREL 5MW has di’¼Ćerent airfoils along the blade, ŌåĄstall is not constant.
Figure 4.50 to 4.52 present the cycle-averaged ŌåĄalong the blade during a surging cycle based
on ŽåS for the surging cases, and < ŌåĄ> for the ’¼üxed case are also presented for comparison.
Note that cases presented here are all under laminar in’¼éow conditions. It is clear that < ŌåĄ>
for the ’¼üxed case can be considered as constants, and stalling did not occur. While for the
surging cases, it is clear that patterns of < ŌåĄ> for cases with same V are almost identical,
and stalling e’¼Ćects are more prominent with higher V. Together with with Equation 2.26, it
is clear that stalling e’¼Ćects occur the most when the rotor is moving against the in’¼éow, as
V0,app & Vn,app has bigger values. Note that stalling mostly occurs at the sections closer to
the root, this is as expected since ŌåĄclose to the root is more subjected to Vn,app due to the
fact that Ōī”r is smaller (Equation 2.18). Moreover, since that timings of stalling are aligned
with the dips of < CT > and < CP > for the surging cases (Figure 4.18), it can be conclude
that stalling is the cause of the dips on the curves.

4.6. Summary and Conclusions
4.5.5
Blockage E’¼Ćects of Di’¼Ćerent Surging Settings
Figure 4.53 and 4.54 show the streamwise pro’¼üles of u3w at y/D = 0, z/D = 0.5 for cases
with di’¼Ćerent AS and !S under both laminar and turbulent (TI = 5.3%) in’¼éow conditions
(groups S.3-S.6). u3w indicates the time-averaged vertical transports of the aerodynamic
power that the ’¼éows contain. And here, positive indicates losses of aerodynamic power from
the wake regions, while negative indicates gains. It can be seen that the power loss due to
the blockages e’¼Ćects are most pronounced for the case with ’¼üxed rotor, and become less
for the cases with bigger V.
This has already been hinted by the behaviours of CT for
di’¼Ćerent surging settings, and the signi’¼ücant drops about u3w for cases with V = 0.44 may
be related to the stalling behaviours discussed previously. Milder blockage e’¼Ćects may have
also contribute for the bigger uDisk of wake. Moreover, both Figure 4.53 and 4.54 shows
that turbulent cases have a negative pro’¼üles of u3w after the rotor, indicating aerodynamic
power is entrained to the wake regions. While for the laminar cases, pro’¼üles of u3w are a
bit irregular, but generally negative for surging cases. This may also related to the highly
repeatable wake systems and the asymmetries for the laminar cases discussed previously.

4.6
Summary and Conclusions
In this chapter, comprehensive investigations about the in’¼éuences of in’¼éow turbulence
intensities TI, surging amplitudes AS, and surging frequencies !S on a surging horizontal-
axis wind turbine rotor had been conducted. In total, 22 cases using LES with ALM had
been carried out and summarized in Table 4.1 & 4.2. Major discoveries and conclusions are
listed below.
1. For cases with laminar in’¼éow conditions, distinct coherent structures were found in
the wakes (PLSB). Moreover, periodic vortical structures were formed through the

interactions and merging of tip-vorticies. The ’¼éows get in and come out of wakes regions
by circulating these vortical structures, which facilitate the momentum exchange of
wake and has positive e’¼Ćects on recovering uDsik of wake.
2. For the laminar in’¼éow cases, mean area-averaged wake velocities uDisk for the surging
cases may be 23% higher when compare to the ’¼üxed cases at x/D = 5.0, the increase
was diminished to around 0.5% ŌćĀ2.0% for turbulent in’¼éow cases.
3. At this point, for the turbulent in’¼éow cases, the increase of mean disk-averaged wake
velocities uDisk for the surging cases may be mainly related to the enhanced mixing
(momentum entrainment) and milder blockage e’¼Ćects, and the extent of increments
are mainly related to V. However, contribution of the two e’¼Ćects are not investigated
in great details in this thesis.
4. In general, in’¼éow TI has little e’¼Ćects on CT and CP. However, V considered in this
chapter a’¼Ćects CT noticeably and slightly a’¼Ćects CP. For the cases considered in this
chapter, before severe stalling occur (V ’Ż┐0.22), higher V brings lower CT with slightly
higher CP. While for cases with severe stalling V = 0.44, CT is signi’¼ücantly lower than
the ’¼üxed case while CP is slightly lower.
5. With the current numerical frameworks, the wake system for surging cases with laminar
in’¼éow conditions are highly periodic and repeatable. That is, the instantaneous ’¼üelds
and phase-locked averaged ’¼üelds are almost identical as long as the considered ŽåS is
same.
6. For cases with turbulent in’¼éow conditions, the instantaneous ’¼üelds are dominated by
the in’¼éow turbulence. However, distinct periodic structures (PLSB) are revealed if
phase-locked averaging is conducted. That is, in’¼éow turbulence dilutes the e’¼Ćects of
surging on wake systems.
Page 96 of 244
==PAGE:128==
4.6. Summary and Conclusions
7. There is slight hysteresis (dynamic in’¼éow) e’¼Ćects for surging cases, considering < CT >
and < CP > follow similar curve while V0,app (VWT) grows and drops during a surge
cycle (Figure 4.19); hysteresis e’¼Ćects are more noticeable with higher !S. Moreover,
stalling a’¼Ćects the curves of < CT > and < CP >, and the extent of stalling closely
related to V.
In the following chapter, another rotor will be added in the downstream, and analysis
about wake interactions with di’¼Ćerent surging-’¼üxed combinations will be conducted; and the
e’¼Ćects of the slight increase about mean disk-averaged streamwise velocity on the downstream
rotor will be tested.

Chapter 5
Results and Discussions of Dual Rotors
Cases
5.1
Introduction
The wake interactions of two uncontrolled full scale wind turbine rotors operating in tan-
dem with di’¼Ćerent surging settings, separation distances between the two rotors, and in’¼éow
turbulence intensities are examined in this chapter. It should be highlighted that complete
knowledge of wake interactions between FOWT is still lacking in the literature, despite the
fact that planning ’¼éoating o’¼Ćshore wind farms will likely require through understanding of
it [9].
Several key aspects of this chapter are described as follow. Firstly, studying the e’¼Ćects
of surging on wake interactions of the two rotors, where cases from none of the rotors are
surging to both being surging are investigated. Next, e’¼Ćects of phase di’¼Ćerences between the
two surging motions ŌłåŽåS0 for the two surging rotors are visited (Figure 1.7). Thirdly, e’¼Ćects
of di’¼Ćerent turbulence in’¼éow intensities on the wake interactions between the two ’¼üxed or
two surging rotors are looked into. Note that all of the aspects mentioned above are tested
with cases having di’¼Ćerent separation distances between the two rotors ŌłåD in order to also
understand the impacts of ŌłåD on wake interactions.
In this chapter, the meshes used and the boundaries conditions applied are identical as
the single rotor cases in chapter 4 (note turbulent cases and laminar cases use similar but
di’¼Ćerent meshes), and rotor of NREL 5MW baseline turbine is again used, so as the settings
for the upstream rotor (except for ŽåS0). That is, the neutral position of the upstream rotor
pup
R0 is kept at x/D = 0 and the rotational speed Ōī”up is kept to be Ōī”rated = 1.27 rad/s (the
superscripts indicate whether the parameter is for the upstream rotor or the downstream
rotor). The interested separation distances between the two rotors in tandem ŌłåD are chosen
to be 3D & 5D.
Though 3D & 5D are smaller or at the lower end of the typical ŌłåD
for realistic o’¼Ćshore wind farms (ŌćĀ7D), they still fall into the interested range for the
studies about wake interaction between wind turbines [17, 20, 95, 96, 97]; moreover, e’¼Ćects
of surging conformations and other parameters on the rotors are likely to be larger with
smaller ŌłåD, and thus smaller ŌłåD may help to identify their roles of wake interactions [20].

to D.8. ŌłåŽåS0 is interested because motions of FOWT are not likely always being in-phase
or out-of-phase, and thus whether ŌłåŽåS0 will a’¼Ćect wake interactions is important, as it may
play a role when designing layouts for ’¼éoating o’¼Ćshore wind farms.

’¼üxed (FF) and surging (SS) rotors, and cases with ŌłåD = 3D & 5D are considered.

conditions, the time instant for the instantaneous snapshots of ’¼üelds are all taken at t =

are identical to the ones for single rotor cases with turbulent in’¼éow conditions in chapter 4.
It should be noticed that the statistics of the downstream rotors are less converged than the
upstream ones. Again, see Appendix C for more details about the sampling and statistic
methods and convergence tests.

5.2. Combinations of Fixed and Surging with ŌłåD being 3D
5.2
Combinations of Fixed and Surging with ŌłåD being
3D Under Laminar and Turbulent In’¼éow Conditions
This section investigates how surging a’¼Ćects the wake interactions of two NREL 5MW
rotor in tandem with ŌłåD being 3D under both laminar and turbulent in’¼éow conditions.
That is, pdown
R0
in Equation 5.2 is 3D. Eight cases are analyzed, and they are cases 31-34 &
38-41 in Table 5.1, corresponding to groups D.1 & D.2 in section 5.1.
5.2.1
Summarizing Wake Characteristics
Characteristics of uDisk for the eight cases (both laminar and turbulent cases) are sum-
marized in Figure 5.3, while the u pro’¼üles are displayed in Figure 5.4 & 5.5 for the four
laminar cases & the four turbulent cases. It is very clear that uDisk do not experience any
signi’¼ücant recovery before hitting the downstream rotor for all the eight cases. Furthermore,
they show that the pro’¼üles of uDisk and u for the laminar cases are mostly dominated by
whether the upstream rotor is surging or ’¼üxed, while the roles of the downstream rotor are
not as signi’¼ücant; but in general, uDisk still recovers faster when the downstream rotor is
surging for the cases with laminar in’¼éow conditions. As for the turbulent cases, the four
cases share very similar pro’¼üles for uDisk and u. While looking closer at x/D = 5, values
of uDisk will be slightly lower for the cases with ’¼üxed upstream rotor compare to the cases
having surging upstream rotor. While as x/D > 5, trends of how uDisk related to surging
conformations become unclear for the turbulent cases as their di’¼Ćerences are less pronounced.
Moreover, if viewing Figure 5.3 together with Figure 5.31 in the later section, seems that
Žåup
S0 also a’¼Ćects pro’¼üles of uDisk with ŌłåD = 3D. Note that the wake systems of the two
rotors are very complex, factors such as turbulence, stalling, and convection of PLSB a’¼Ćect
the systems. As for the u pro’¼üles, laminar cases have irregular shapes while turbulent cases
have Gaussian shape, just as the cases of single rotor in the previous chapter.
5.2.2
Summarizing Rotor Performances
Power coe’¼ācients for the upstream and downstream rotors under laminar in’¼éow condi-
tions are summarized in Figure 5.6, while the counterparts for the cases with turbulent in’¼éow
conditions are in Figure 5.7. Note that the calculations of CP (and CT as well) for the both
rotor are based on reference wind speed being 11.4 m/s. Together with the < CP > curves
for the single rotor (Figure 4.4), it can be easily concluded that the performances of < CP >
are (almost) not a’¼Ćected by the rotor at downstream, and values for CP & C
up
P in Table 4.1
& Table 5.1 also tell the same tale (still very slight blockages can be detected by comparing
C
(up)
P
, Figure 6.10 in the later chapter gives a better visualization). As for the downstream
rotor, curves of < Cdown
P
> for the four laminar cases are signi’¼ücantly di’¼Ćerent, while the
turbulent cases share almost the same curves (only depending on the surging conformation
of the downstream rotor). However, as looking G

in Table 5.1 for the turbulent cases,
one can ’¼ünd that C
down

for the case SF is 1.6% higher comparing to case FF; this pointed

5.2. Combinations of Fixed and Surging with ŌłåD being 3D
5.2.3
Velocity and TKE Fields
The instantaneous contours of u for the eight cases with two rotors separated with
ŌłåD = 3D are in Figure 5.9. For both the laminar and turbulent cases, the instantaneous
’¼üelds are more dominated by the upstream rotor. For example, u ’¼üelds after the second
rotor of cases FF and FS with laminar in’¼éow conditions (case 31 & 32) are rather similar,
which the e’¼Ćects of surging of downstream rotor are much less pronounced compared to the
upstream rotor, and same can be said by comparing cases SF and SS with laminar in’¼éow
conditions (cases 33 & 34). Moreover, similar with the cases of single rotor in chapter 4, the
instantaneous ’¼üelds of the turbulent cases are dominated by the in’¼éow turbulence, as can be
seen that the u ’¼üelds for cases 38-41 share similar features. However, if looking closer, one
can say that wake structure of cases 38 & 39 (FF & FS) share more detail features between
themselves, and same can be said for cases 40 & 41 (SF & SS), and these suggest that the
in’¼éuences of the upstream rotor still exist and being more pronounced than the downstream
one.
Figure 5.10 displays the < u >0ŌćĪ’¼üelds of the eight cases. Note that the phase-locked
relationships are maintained even with the introduced of the downstream rotor, it is main-
tained with the rotational speed of the downstream rotor being 3Ōī”down = 2Ōī”up = !S (note
the 120ŌŚ”symmetry of the rotor (3P)) for all the eight cases. Similar with the single rotor
cases, the ’¼üelds of u and < u >0ŌćĪfor the four laminar cases are almost identical up until
x = 4.5D, and this can also be explained with the <TKE>0ŌćĪ’¼üelds presented later. As for
the turbulent cases, surging e’¼Ćects of the upstream rotor can be easily identi’¼üed by observing
< u >0ŌćĪ’¼üelds. However, surging e’¼Ćects of the downstream rotor cannot be clearly detected
by the phase-locked averaged streamwise velocity ’¼üelds < u >0ŌćĪ, and ’¼üelds of < Ōłåp >0ŌćĪ&
< !y >0ŌćĪpresented later also show similar things.
The ’¼üelds of u are displayed in Figure 5.11. Clearly that u is further slowed down
by the downstream rotor for all the eight cases.
For the laminar cases, unlike with the
cases of single ’¼üxed rotor in chapter 4, all the four cases have signi’¼ücant recovery of u after
x = 6D, suggesting wake-wake interaction promotes recovery of u under laminar in’¼éow
conditions. Moreover, the recovery rates of u for the laminar cases are dominated by the
surging conformations of the upstream rotor, and unsurprisingly, u recovered faster while
upstream rotor is surging. As for the four turbulent cases, they share very similar u ’¼üelds even
the surging-’¼üxed conformations are di’¼Ćerent, which is similar to that the u ’¼üelds are similar
for single rotor cases with di’¼Ćerent surging settings in the previous chapter (Figure E.7).
The ’¼üelds <TKE>0ŌćĪfor the eight cases are shown in Figure 5.12. For the laminar
cases, even with the addition of the downstream rotor, low values of <TKE>0ŌćĪ’¼üelds were
still maintained at the vicinity of the downstream rotor, and this is still true with case
SS. However, unlike with the single rotor cases (Figure 4.21), low values of <TKE>0ŌćĪdid
not persist to the far downstream for the four cases, signi’¼ücant ’¼éuctuations unrelated to
surging nor rotations of rotors occurred around x = 4.5D, suggesting the breakdown of the
wake structures. Once again, the patterns of <TKE>0ŌćĪ’¼üelds are more dominated by the
upstream rotor. Interestingly, just as the single rotor cases, values of <TKE>0ŌćĪ’¼üelds are
lower for the cases with the upstream rotor being surging, suggesting surging actually makes
the wake systems more orderly (repeatable). As for the turbulent cases, other than the two
Page 107 of 244
==PAGE:139==
5.2. Combinations of Fixed and Surging with ŌłåD being 3D
di’¼Ćerent patterns around 0 ’Ż┐x/D ’Ż┐3 depending whether the upstream rotor is surging
(related to the PLSB structures of upstream rotor), the four cases share similar features.
The ’¼üelds of TKE are shown in Figure 5.13. For the laminar cases, similar with the u
’¼üelds, TKE ’¼üelds are mostly a’¼Ćected by whether the upstream rotor is surging. Furthermore,
unlike the single rotor cases, where only at the tracks of the tip vorticies have signi’¼ücant
TKE values, now most of the parts within the rotor projection areas (ŌłÆ0.5 ’Ż┐z/D ’Ż┐0.5)
have pronounced values, especially for cases FF and FS. As for the four turbulent cases, the
addition of the downstream rotor increased TKE of the wakes, which is unsurprisingly. And
just as the u ’¼üelds, the four turbulent cases share very similar features.
5.2.4
Pressure and Vorticity Fields
Fields of < Ōłåp >0ŌćĪand Ōłåp for the eight cases are displayed in Figure 5.14 and 5.15
(instantaneous ’¼üelds Ōłåp is in Figure F.1).
The phase-locked averaged ’¼üelds < Ōłåp >0ŌćĪ
show that upstream rotor in’¼éuence < Ōłåp >0ŌćĪ’¼üelds much more than the downstream rotor,
especially for the turbulent cases where the e’¼Ćects of the downstream rotor are almost not
observable. Ōłåp ’¼üelds show that although they are a’¼Ćected by the surging conformations for
the laminar cases, they are almost the same for the turbulent cases.
The ’¼üelds of < !y >0ŌćĪfor the eight cases are in Figure 5.16, the ’¼üelds depict the
characteristics of the released tip vorticies of the two wind turbine rotors in tandem (’¼üelds
of < !x >0ŌćĪ& < !z >0ŌćĪare in Figure F.2 & F.3). For the laminar cases, case FF shows
that the vortex tubes of the two rotor are separated around the vicinity of the downstream
rotor, and they eventually merge together and smear out in the more downstream regions
(x Ōēź5D). Case FS with laminar in’¼éow conditions shares very similar features with case
FF, while the periodic structures due to surging of the downstream rotor exist, and those
structures do make the stream tube of the upstream rotor interacts with the wake system
of upstream rotor earlier. As for the cases SF and cases SS under laminar in’¼éow conditions,
the vortex tube or periodic structures result from the downstream rotor are not found,
suggesting the strong induction ’¼üelds due to the surging upstream rotor dominates over
the one of the downstream rotor. Furthermore, an interesting phenomena is that after the
interactions of the two wakes from upstream and downstream rotor, the values of < !y >0ŌćĪ
’¼üelds for the four laminar cases are smeared out, making them similar to the turbulent
cases. For the four turbulent cases, the ’¼üelds of < !y >0ŌćĪafter the downstream rotor are
almost indistinguishable, suggesting the e’¼Ćects of surging of the downstream rotor are weak;
however, it is very clear that with ŌłåD = 3D, the downstream rotor situates at the region
where the shapes of periodic structures (PLSB) of the surging upstream rotors are still quite
sharp.
5.2.5
Momentum Entrainment
Fields of ŌłÆ@p/@x, ŌłÆw(@u/@z), and ŌłÆ@u0w0/@z are displayed in Figure 5.17, 5.18, and
5.19. They are the three ’¼üelds contribute the most to the momentum recovery (@uu/@x)
along the streamwise direction (Appendix B). With the ’¼üelds of ŌłÆ@p/@x, it can be seen that
separation of 3D is close enough to make the mean pressure ’¼üelds of the two rotors interact
Page 108 of 244
==PAGE:140==
5.2. Combinations of Fixed and Surging with ŌłåD being 3D
with each other, and this probably explains why the values of C
up
T are (very) slightly lower
compared to the values of CT with just a single rotor. While for the patterns of the ’¼üelds,
other than cases SF and SS with laminar in’¼éow conditions have peculiar patterns right after
the downstream rotor, they behave as one may have expected. While for the ’¼üelds of convec-
tion term ŌłÆw(@u/@z), cases with laminar in’¼éow conditions showcase that their momentum
recovery due to convections are much more pronounced compared to the turbulent cases,
which is similar to the cases with single rotor (Figure 4.14). As for the Reynolds stress term
ŌłÆ@u0w0/@z, cases FF & FS with laminar in’¼éow conditions show that after the interactions
of the wakes of upstream and downstream rotors, their patterns look more similar with the
turbulent cases. While for the cases SS & SF with laminar in’¼éow conditions, they once
more showed the peculiar patterns just as the single rotor cases (Figure 4.15). These suggest
that wake-wake interactions with laminar in’¼éow conditions between bottom-mounted wind
turbines may facilitate turbulent transitions (breakdown of the wake structures) more than
the surging (upstream) rotor, and these might related to that the induction ’¼üelds of surging
rotor are much more persist. As for the turbulent cases, once again, di’¼Ćerences for the four
turbulent cases about ŌłÆ@u0w0/@z ’¼üelds are not obvious.

5.4
Di’¼Ćerent Ōłåup
ŽåS0 with ŌłåD being 3D Under Laminar and
Turbulent In’¼éow Conditions
This section studies the e’¼Ćects of phase di’¼Ćerences between the two surging motions ŌłåŽåS0
on the wake interactions between two surging rotors with ŌłåD = 3D both under laminar and
turbulent in’¼éow conditions. Eight cases are analyzed, which are cases 34-37 and 41-44 in
Table 5.1, and correspond to groups D.5 and D.6 in section 5.1. E’¼Ćects of ŌłåŽåS0 are studied
by running cases with Žåup
S0 being 0.0ŌćĪ, 0.5ŌćĪ, 1.0ŌćĪ, or 1.5ŌćĪwhile keeping Žådown
S0
= 0.0ŌćĪin
Equation 5.1 and 5.2. That is, cases with ŌłåŽåS0 being 0.0ŌćĪ, 0.5ŌćĪ, 1.0ŌćĪ, or 1.5ŌćĪare studied
here (Equation 5.3).
For future o’¼Ćshore ’¼éoating wind farms, whether ŌłåŽåS0 would signi’¼ücantly a’¼Ćect the
wake interactions between FOWTs is critically important when it comes to wind farm layout
design, since it might a’¼Ćect the mode of wake interactions and thus the AEP & LCoE.
However, to the best knowledge of the author, research about studying e’¼Ćects of ŌłåŽåS0 on
wake interactions is still missing. It should be noted that ŌłåŽåS0 is not solely depends on
ŌłåD, but also depends on how fast the waves propagate. In short, ŌłåŽåS0 at least depends on
ŌłåD, hydrodynamics, and sea bed topography; correctly predict ŌłåŽåS0 requires high ’¼üdelity
hydrodynamic simulations and reliable sea states data, which are beyond the scope of this
thesis. For this thesis, only whether ŌłåŽåS0 will a’¼Ćect wake interactions is focused.
5.4.1
Summarizing Wake Characteristics
Characteristics of uDisk for the eight cases are in Figure 5.31, while u pro’¼üles of the four
laminar cases & the other four turbulent cases are in Figure 5.32 & 5.33. It can be seen that
the uDisk are less sensitive to ŌłåŽåS0 than the surging-’¼üxed combinations (section 5.2) for both
laminar and turbulent in’¼éow conditions. However, if looking at u pro’¼üles of the laminar
cases, the four cases still have signi’¼ücant di’¼Ćerences.

with di’¼Ćerent ŌłåŽåS0. As Žådown

5.6. Di’¼Ćerent In’¼éow TI with Dual Rotors being Fixed or Surging
5.6
Di’¼Ćerent In’¼éow Turbulence Intensities with Dual Ro-
tors being Fixed or Surging
This section investigates the in’¼éuences of di’¼Ćerent in’¼éow turbulence intensities on the
wake interactions between two ’¼üxed or surging FOWT rotors, and both ŌłåD = 3D & 5D
are looked into. The interested cases in this section belong to groups D.9, D.10, D.11, &
D.12 in section 5.1 (16 cases in total). Since most features about wake interactions between
two rotors in tandem have been well explored in the previous parts of this chapter, this
section will only cover the aspects where in’¼éow turbulence intensities play signi’¼ücant roles,
including pro’¼üles of uDisk, rotor performances of the downstream rotor, and ’¼üelds of u, TKE,
& < !y >0ŌćĪ.
5.6.1
Summarizing Wake Characteristics
Figure 5.49 & 5.50 display the pro’¼üles of uDisk for the case with ŌłåD = 3D & ŌłåD = 5D;
each ’¼ügure presented the cases with ’¼üxed or surging rotors with di’¼Ćerent in’¼éow TI. In
general, the trends are comparable with the cases having only a single rotor (Figure 4.2).
First, with laminar in’¼éow conditions, recovery rates of uDisk for surging cases are much larger
than the ’¼üxed cases, and this may relate to the ’¼éows of the surging cases go in and come out
the wake around the PLSB, which facilitate wake recovery by mixing. Second, for the cases
with turbulent in’¼éow conditions, pro’¼üles of uDisk for cases with higher in’¼éow TI seems to be
larger for the surging cases than the ’¼üxed cases, and the di’¼Ćerences between cases with TI
= 2.7% & 5.3% once again seem to become smaller as going downstream. Moreover, pro’¼üles
of uDisk for ’¼üxed and surging cases with same TI become much more similar when comparing
to the laminar cases, with the surging cases having slightly larger uDisk for most of the cases.
5.6.2
Summarizing Rotor Performances
Figure 5.51 & 5.52 present < CP > of the upstream and downstream rotor for the ’¼üxed
& surging cases with ŌłåD = 3D under di’¼Ćerent in’¼éow TI, while Figure 5.53 & 5.54 present
the counterparts for cases with ŌłåD = 5D.
These ’¼ügures clearly showed that < Cup
P
>
is insensitive to ŌłåD and in’¼éow TI, which agrees with the previous results. While for the
downstream rotors, generally cases with higher in’¼éow TI have larger < Cdown
P
>, both for the
’¼üxed or surging cases, suggesting the e’¼Ćective in’¼éow velocities seen by the downstream rotors
get bigger with larger in’¼éow TI. Moreover, the extents of stalling of the downstream rotors
di’¼Ćer with di’¼Ćerent in’¼éow TI, especially for the surging cases with ŌłåD = 5D (Figure 5.54b),
and this should relate to the di’¼Ćerent e’¼Ćective in’¼éow velocities seen by the downstream rotor
(di’¼Ćerent Vdown). It should be noted that Ōī”down for all cases in this chapter is prescribed
with same value (Ōī”down = 0.84 rad/s).
As for C
down
P
(listed in Table 5.1), again it is clear that larger TI gives bigger C
down
P
for
all ’¼üxed & surging cases with ŌłåD = 3D & ŌłåD = 5D. However, as comparing C
down
P
between
a pair of ’¼üxed and surging cases with same in’¼éow TI & same ŌłåD, it is hard to tell whether
’¼üxed or surging case gives bigger C
down
P
.
The reasons behind this may be complicated,

5.6. Di’¼Ćerent In’¼éow TI with Dual Rotors being Fixed or Surging
5.6.3
Summarizing the Field Data
Figure 5.55 to 5.57 present the ’¼üelds of u, TKE, & < !y >0ŌćĪfor ’¼üxed and surging cases
with ŌłåD = 3D under di’¼Ćerent in’¼éow TI, and Figure 5.58 to 5.60 present the counterparts
for cases with ŌłåD = 5D. Figure 5.55 & 5.58 show that the u ’¼üelds are generally larger for
the cases with higher in’¼éow TI, while the general features for the turbulent cases are not
altered by the strength of in’¼éow TI. With the TKE ’¼üelds displayed by Figure 5.56 & 5.59,
it can be seen that the background TKE generally comply with in’¼éow TI, and the wake
regions feature higher TI ’¼üelds. However, a very interesting phenomenon is that the TKE
’¼üelds after the downstream rotor for the cases with higher in’¼éow TI are not higher than the
cases with lower in’¼éow TI; this is most observable with the surging cases, which cases with
in’¼éow TI = 2.7% have higher TKE ’¼üelds after the downstream rotors than cases having
5.3% & 11.6% for both ŌłåD = 3D & ŌłåD = 5D. The reasons behind this may related to
the interactions between vortical structures and ambient turbulence. Ambient turbulence
may trigger the breakdown process of vortical structures (PLSB) by introducing additional
instabilities, resulting in higher turbulence intensity; however, if the turbulence intensity is
so high that distorted the vortical structures generated by the wind turbine rotors too fast,
the breakdown process may be terminated earlier before it could bring additional turbulence.
The above-mentioned idea may be supported by the observation that cases with TI = 2.7%
have higher TKE ’¼üelds for the surging cases than the ’¼üxed cases after the downstream
rotor, while cases with TI = 5.3% & TI = 11.6% have similar TKE ’¼üelds for the surging
and ’¼üxed cases; note that surging cases convected large and strong vortical structures after
the downstream rotors (see Figure 5.57 & 5.60). Figure 5.57 & 5.60 present the < !y >0ŌćĪ
’¼üelds. Similar with the cases with single rotor under di’¼Ćerent turbulent in’¼éow conditions
(Figure 4.12), the vortical structures related to PLSB become less noticeable with higher
in’¼éow TI.

5.7. Angle of Attack and Stalling of the Downstream Rotor
5.7
Angle of Attack and Stalling of the Downstream Ro-
tor
This section dedicates to explain stalling of the downstream rotor via analyzing its
pro’¼üles of angle of attack ŌåĄdown due to the e’¼Ćects of surging and wake interactions. The
selected cases to analyze are the turbulent cases with in’¼éow TI being 5.3% and separation
distances ŌłåD being 3D or 5D, and they belong to group D.2, D.4, & D.6 introduced in
section 5.1. Note that AS = 4 m and !S = 0.63 rad/s are the surging settings for all the
surging cases in this section.
Figure 5.62 and 5.63 present the cycle-averaged ŌåĄdown along the blade spans for the cases
of di’¼Ćerent surging-’¼üxed combinations and di’¼Ćerent ŌłåŽåS0 with ŌłåD = 3D under turbulent
in’¼éow conditions (TI = 5.3%, see subsection 4.5.3 for ŌåĄstall). For comparison, Figure 5.61
presents < ŌåĄup > along the blade span for case FF and SS with in’¼éow TI = 5.3% and
ŌłåD = 3D. Note that pro’¼üles of < ŌåĄup > in Figure 5.61 are very similar to the pro’¼üles of
< ŌåĄ> for cases with single rotor in Figure 4.50, despite the in’¼éow conditions are changed
from laminar to turbulent.
It is worth noting that the rotational speed of the downstream rotor here Ōī”down is smaller
than Ōī”up and the estimated in’¼éow velocity for the downstream rotor V down
0
(7.6 m/s) is
smaller than V up
0
(11.4 m/s) (the estimated tip speed ratio of the downstream rotors ╬╗down
are same as ╬╗up = 7, but note that reference velocities for CT & CP are 11.4 m/s). However,
by comparing < ŌåĄdown > (Figure 5.62a) and < ŌåĄup > (Figure 5.61a) for the case FF with
ŌłåD = 3D (case 38), it can be seen that < ŌåĄdown > is darker in blue, suggesting V down
0
is a
bit over estimated based on the velocity triangle.
As it can be seen by comparing < ŌåĄup > & < ŌåĄdown > of the surging rotors with
ŌłåD = 3D in Figure 5.63 (< ŌåĄdown >) with Figure 5.61b (< ŌåĄup >), despite having exactly
same AS and !S, < ŌåĄdown > in Figure 5.63 displays deeper stalling for the downstream
surging rotors when comparing with the ones in the upstream in Figure 5.61b. This is as
expected since that the in’¼éow angle of the downstream rotor Žådown is more susceptible to
the surging velocity of the downstream rotor V down
WT
(since the e’¼Ćective Vdown is bigger),
and this can be explained by the velocity triangle (Figure 2.1) and Equation 2.17 & 2.18.
Notice that V down
0
is deemed to be over estimated for ŌłåD = 3D, thus more severe stalling
of the surging downstream rotors for cases 41-44 could not be due to the under estimation
of V down
0
. Regarding the possibility of more severe stalling for the rotor operating in other
FOWTŌĆÖs wakes, controlling strategy of FOWT will be critically important for AEP of the
future ’¼éoating wind farms.
Looking closer to < ŌåĄdown > in Figure 5.63, it can be seen that case ŌłåŽåS0 = 0.5ŌćĪ
experienced the most severe stalling of the four cases. This may due to that the downstream
rotor of case ŌłåŽåS0 = 0.5ŌćĪ(case 42) move against the in’¼éow while being right between the
PLSB (see Figure 5.38 and 5.40), making it has the highest cycle-averaged V down
0,app ; and thus
leads to the most severe stalling, which can be con’¼ürmed with < ŌåĄdown > in Figure 5.63. As
one may have expected, stalling has negative impacts on the power conversion rates of wind
turbine rotors, and this can explain why case ŌłåŽåS0 = 0.5ŌćĪhas slightly lower C
down
P
when
comparing to the other three cases with di’¼Ćerent ŌłåŽåS0.

5.8. Summary and Conclusions
5.8
Summary and Conclusions
In this chapter, comprehensive investigations about wake interactions between two ’¼üxed
or surging full scale wind turbine rotors in tandem were conducted. The studied parame-
ters include the surging-’¼üxed conformations (FF, FS, SF, & SS), phase di’¼Ćerences of the
two surging motions (ŌłåŽåS0), in’¼éow turbulence intensities (TI), and the separation distance
(spacing) between the two rotors (ŌłåD). In’¼éow conditions of laminar and turbulent with TI
= 5.3% are most focused. As for the surging cases, AS = 4 m/s & !S = 0.63 rad/s are set.
The major discoveries and conclusions for this chapter are listed below.
1. Besides from the ’¼üelds of phase-locked averaged quantities, the instantaneous and time-
averaged ’¼üelds for two rotors in tandem are not signi’¼ücantly altered by the surging
conformations as the in’¼éow conditions are turbulent (TI = 2.7, 5.3 or 11.6%, see
section 5.2 & 5.6), suggesting the modes of wake interactions between the two rotors
are similar either them being ’¼üxed or surging as the in’¼éow conditions are turbulent.
2. With the facts that the power performances of the downstream rotor in Table 5.1
become slightly higher if the upstream rotor is surging rather than being ’¼üxed (opera-
tional conditions of the downstream rotor should be the same for comparison), it can
be suggested that surging (of the upstream rotor) do facilitate wake recovery; however,
the e’¼Ćects are much less pronounced with the realistic turbulent in’¼éow conditions (1-
2% gains for both ŌłåD = 3D & 5D) compared to the laminar in’¼éow conditions (ŌćĀ10%
gains for ŌłåD = 3D, ŌćĀ40% gains for ŌłåD = 5D).
3. If the surging motions for both upstream and downstream rotor are same (in sense of
AS & !S), the power performances (stalling e’¼Ćects) of the downstream rotor is more
subjected to the in’¼éuences of surging motions compare to the upstream rotor in the
sense of relative ’¼éuctuation amplitudes of CP. This is due to the fact the the e’¼Ćective
ratios between the maximum surging speed and the in’¼éow wind speed seen by the
downstream rotor (Vdown) will be larger than the upstream one.
4. The instantaneous wake structures of the two rotor are more dominated by the surging
conformations (’¼üxed or surging) of the upstream rotor, and the PLSB of the down-
stream rotor are much less clearer compare to the one of the upstream rotor.
5. PLSB of the upstream rotor do slightly a’¼Ćects the power performance of the down-
stream rotor with the realistic turbulent in’¼éow conditions (TI = 5.3%) when the inter-
distance between the rotors is rather close (ŌłåD = 3D), this can be con’¼ürmed with
the fact that the phase angle di’¼Ćerences between the surge motions for the two rotors
(ŌłåŽåS0) a’¼Ćects the power performance of the downstream rotor. However, the e’¼Ćects
of PLSB will signi’¼ücantly diminish with larger ŌłåD (ŌłåD = 5D, note that 5D is rather
small compare to the typical ŌłåD in the real o’¼Ćshore wind farms), and thus the e’¼Ćects
of ŌłåŽåS0 become less important and even negligible.
6. Since that the e’¼Ćects of ŌłåŽåS0 (phase angle di’¼Ćerences between the surge motions for
the two rotors) on the wake interactions between the two surging rotor are rather weak,
Page 161 of 244
==PAGE:193==
5.8. Summary and Conclusions
the phase angle of surging may not be a crucial factor to consider for wake interactions
between rotors of FOWT as the inter-distance is large enough (ŌłåD Ōēź5). This may
drastically lower the degree of freedoms when assessing the AEP numerically for the
’¼éoating o’¼Ćshore wind farms.
7. Wake-wake interactions for cases with laminar in’¼éow conditions di’¼Ćer very signi’¼ücantly
to the cases with turbulent in’¼éow conditions. For the laminar cases, the recovery rates
of uDisk are heavily in’¼éuenced by the parameters inputted to the simulations (such as
the surging settings of the rotors), and the downstream rotor will trigger the breakdown
processes (turbulent transitions). While for the cases with turbulent in’¼éow conditions,
their results are much less sensitive to the input parameters (such as surging-’¼üxed
conformations & ŌłåŽåS0), even with TI which is quite low (2.7%). Since that laminar
in’¼éow conditions are unrealistic out in the ’¼üelds, thus if the goal is to properly simulate
wake interactions of FOWTs operating under the real world conditions, turbulent in’¼éow
conditions can be deemed as a requirement.
8. Prescribed constant rotational speeds (Ōī”up & Ōī”down) for both upstream and down-
stream rotor may not be the best way for modelling the wake interactions for multiple
wind turbine rotors out in ’¼üelds, especially for FOWT in motions, since that the oper-
ational conditions of the rotor will be quite o’¼Ćfrom the optimal condition (especially
for the downstream one), which will not be the case for modern wind turbine with
proper controller.
To better understand the full potential of enhancing power outputs for surging rotors
in tandem, cases of dual rotors with controller are presented in the next chapter, and some
of the cases are compared with the cases in this chapter.
Page 162 of 244
==PAGE:194==
Chapter 6
Results and Discussions of Cases with
Simple Controller
6.1
Introduction
For cases in previous chapters, no controlling mechanisms were applied on the rotor of
NREL 5MW baseline turbine. Although this con’¼üguration is more straightforward and would
make the systems simpler to analyze, this is not the actual use case for contemporary multi-
megawatt wind turbines. Setup without controller makes the rotor operates with sub-optimal
conditions (╬╗ which is not optimal), possibly making ŌćĀCP in Equation 4.1 substantially
lower; moreover, this setup also ends up in severe stalling with some surging settings (see
subsection 4.5.4), and thus lowering the power converting rates (ŌćĀCP ). Therefore, to better
understand the full potential of ’¼éoating wind farms, a simple controller (MPPT tracking)
described in section 2.7 is added and implemented into the simulation framework used in
previous chapters, hoping to make the overall power output (CP) higher.
Note that when adjusting the generator torque Ōī¦Gen with Equation 2.34 during the
simulations in this chapter, CP,opt = 0.5177 and ╬╗opt = 7.00 are set, which are based on
the CP of case 1 in Table 4.1 (’¼üxed, laminar in’¼éow conditions). Even though the rated
conditions are not the optimal conditions in sense of power conversion rate (not exactly
optimal but should be close to), it is chosen to make the comparisons with the cases in the
previous chapters more straightforward. Values of I (total rotational inertia), IR (inertia
of rotor), IGen (inertia of drive train), IB (inertia of single blade), and Ihub (inertia of hub)
for NREL 5MW baseline turbine are listed in Table 6.1, they are documented in the report
of NREL 5MW [62]. Note that parallel axis theorem was applied to obtain the value of IB
from with respect to blade root to with respect to the rotor center, and IGen is respect to
low speed shaft.
Table 6.1: Values of moment inertia for di’¼Ćerent parts of NREL 5MW baseline turbine.

di’¼Ćerent conditions (in’¼éow velocities V0) in section G.1, where the controller was turned

well. Also, the inditial tests show that the time needed for the controller to reach its new
quasi-steady states after turning it on is much longer than the surging period TS considered

better estimate the performance of the controller.

6.3. Summarizing Rotor Performances
6.3.3
Compare with Cases without Controller
To estimate the e’¼Ćectiveness of controller on rotor performances, pairs of cases with
same settings but one with controller and one without are compared. Note that only single
(the upstream) rotor is focused at this point, since that with the controller introduced, there
will be too many variables in’¼éuencing the performance of the downstream rotor operating
under wake conditions. And to reduce computational resources, upstream rotor for cases
with ŌłåD = 5D (cases 85-88) are considered as operating alone, since that blockage e’¼Ćects

monotonic, seems that the reaction speed of the controller and stalling a’¼Ćect the values.

6.3.4
Angle of Attack
Figure 6.7 & 6.8 display the cycle-averaged angle of attack of the upstream & down-
stream rotors (< ŌåĄup > & < ŌåĄdown >) for case with ŌłåD = 3D (cases 81-84). By comparing
< ŌåĄup > (Figure 6.7) with < ŌåĄ> of the single rotor cases without controller in subsec-
tion 4.5.4 (the upstream rotor is assumed to be not a’¼Ćected by the downstream rotor), it
can be seen that even though the goal of the controller is to alleviate the e’¼Ćects of stalling by
’¼üxing ŌåĄthrough regulating Ōī”, extents of stalling e’¼Ćects of the upstream rotor for cases with
ŌłåD = 3D are almost una’¼Ćected; this is related to the fact that I for the rotor is too big for
the controller to properly adjust Ōī”. In section G.2, < ŌåĄup > for the case with much smaller I
is displayed and shows that the stalling e’¼Ćects are much cured. As for the downstream rotor
(< ŌåĄdown > in Figure 6.8), stalling e’¼Ćects are more signi’¼ücant than the upstream rotor for
the surging cases, the reason behind it may be that the e’¼Ćective V seen by the downstream
rotor is bigger than the upstream one, just as the cases without controller in chapter 5.
For the cases with ŌłåD = 5D (cases 85-88), only < ŌåĄdown > are displayed (Figure 6.9)
since < ŌåĄup > for these cases are almost identical to cases with ŌłåD = 3D. It should be
noted that except for the case with AS = 2 m (case 86), behaviours of < ŌåĄdown > for cases
with ŌłåD = 5D are very similar with ŌłåD = 3D, only that the surging cases with ŌłåD = 5D
have less stalling e’¼Ćects than cases with ŌłåD = 3D; probably due to that the e’¼Ćective V seen
by the downstream rotor of cases with ŌłåD = 5D is smaller than with ŌłåD = 3D, since V down
0
is more recovered for cases with ŌłåD = 5D. However, very interestingly, < ŌåĄdown > for the
case with AS = 2 m and ŌłåD = 5D (Figure 6.9b) almost does not experienced stalling, even
though < ŌåĄup > does (Figure 6.7b), where the upstream rotor indeed has smaller e’¼Ćective
V; the actual reason behind this is unclear to author. However, it should be bear in mind
that the system dynamics are actually very complicated and hard to precisely predict and
explain, since there are multiple variables that in’¼éuence the varying process of ŌåĄ, such as

implementation of controller.
Case 52 (FF, ŌłåD = 5D, without controller) is chosen to
be the reference case. By comparing, clearly that for AS = 2 or 4 m, surging motions do
make CP larger, and implementation of controller will also improved the values. However,
as AS = 8 m, both surging motions and implementation of controller has negative impacts
on the power performances, which relate to the reaction speeds and stalling. Moreover, as

e’¼Ćects are also detectable for surging cases with ŌłåD = 3D if comparing to the single rotor

6.5. Summary and Conclusions
6.5
Summary and Conclusions
In this chapter, eight (with another eleven in Appendix G) cases with dual surging or
’¼üxed rotors of NREL 5MW baseline turbine have been conducted with the implementation
of a validated simple torque (MPPT) controller, and the setups & results are summarized in
Table 6.2. All eight cases have realistic turbulent in’¼éow conditions in o’¼Ćshore environment
(TI = 5.3%), and four of them have ŌłåD = 3D, while ŌłåD = 5D for the other four. Four
di’¼Ćerent AS are tested, including one being ’¼üxed (AS = 0 m).
Major discoveries and
conclusions are listed below.
1. With the interested surging settings (AS & !S), the implemented controller are not
able to adjust the operational conditions (Ōī”) of the rotor fast enough to allow it to
operate under the desired conditions. The main reason is the rotational inertia of the
rotor I is quite large.
2. With the large inertia of the rotor, CP,Gen is signi’¼ücantly lagging behind CP,Aero, and
the varying ranges of CP,Gen are smaller than the ones of CP,Aero for the surging cases.
3. Stalling for the surging cases are not able to be cured with the implementation of the
current controller, both for the upstream and downstream rotors.
4. In general, for the controlled cases considered in this chapter, the total power outputted
will be bene’¼üted with moderate surging motions (positive surGCP ), while there might
be losses if surging e’¼Ćects lead to severe stalling.
5. Overall, the bene’¼üts of the current controller on CP (conGCP ) depend quite a lot on
surging settings, and it may be only 1% with the cases considered in this chapter (there
might even be losses), which is much lower than the values estimated with quasi-steady
states predictions CP,Tar.
6. With the implementation of current controller, the strengths of repeating coherent
structures in wakes (PLSB) are magni’¼üed.
The results in this chapter suggested that to achieved better power output for FOWT
subject to motions, more advanced controlling strategies may have to be implemented, while
this topic will be left for future works.
Page 178 of 244
==PAGE:210==
Chapter 7
Conclusions and Recommendations
7.1
Concluding Remarks
This thesis conducted several simulations with high ’¼üdelity CFD method (LES with
ALM) to comprehensively understand the wake structures, wake interactions, and rotors
performances of surging ’¼éoating o’¼Ćshore wind turbine (FOWT) rotors, including cases with
single rotor without controller, cases with dual rotor in tandem without controller, and cases
with dual rotor in tandem with controller. Both laminar and turbulent in’¼éow conditions are
considered, and the surging motions of FOWT in this thesis are prescribed and harmonic.
The simulation framework is described in chapter 3, and it has also been veri’¼üed and val-
idated. The research questions proposed in section 1.3 are answered by analyzing the data
of the simulation cases.
Will surge motions of FOWT signi’¼ücant alter its wake structures
under both laminar and turbulent in’¼éow conditions?
In chapter 4, it is found that surging will introduce periodic structures (PLSB) to the
wake system after the surging FOWT for cases both under laminar and turbulent in’¼éow
conditions; PLSB can be readily observed with instantaneous ’¼üelds for cases under laminar
in’¼éow conditions, while PLSB for cases under turbulent in’¼éow conditions appear after phase-
locked averaging. With the laminar cases, it is found that PLSB are formed by the merging
of tip vorticies. The repeating rates of PLSB are as surging frequency !S, and bigger surging
amplitude AS brings sharper PLSB.
Will the e’¼Ćects of the surging motions on the wakes of FOWT facil-
itate the recovery rates of uDisk?
In chapter 4, it is found that the induction ’¼üelds of the vortical structures (PLSB) re-
leased due to surging facilitate ’¼éows to get in and come out of the wake of FOWT for both
laminar and turbulent cases, and thus may enhance the mixing process and help recovering
uDisk. Indeed, uDisk at the downstream positions for the surging cases with laminar in’¼éow
179
==PAGE:211==
7.2. Outlooks and Recommendations
conditions is signi’¼ücantly increased compare to the ’¼üxed case, but the increase rates are
much less for cases with turbulent in’¼éow conditions, and the increase rates do not have
an obvious correlation with in’¼éow TI; however, for both laminar and turbulent cases, the
increase rates all have positive correlations with V.
Additionally, in chapter 4, for the time-averaged power performances of the rotor CP,
it is found that it has positive correlations with V if the rotor does not experience severe
stalling during surging cycle. Moreover, surging rotors have lower values of CT.
How do the surging motions a’¼Ćect the wake interactions between
two wind turbine rotors?
In chapter 5, it is found out that surging conformations do a’¼Ćect the wake interactions
between two rotors in tandem a lot with laminar in’¼éow conditions.
However, with the
turbulent in’¼éow conditions having realistic TI (5.3%), whether the rotors are surging do not
change the modes of wake interactions signi’¼ücantly. It is also ’¼ünd out ŌłåŽåS0 may slightly
a’¼Ćect the power performance of the downstream rotor under turbulent in’¼éow conditions with
rather small inter-distance between rotors ŌłåD (3D), while the e’¼Ćects of ŌłåŽåS0 diminish and
become even negligible with larger ŌłåD (5D). In general, surging do have positive e’¼Ćects on
power performances for the system with two rotors, both making CP itself and uDisk larger,
as long as stalling e’¼Ćects are not too severe. Furthermore, results in this chapter show that
for same surging settings, performances of rotor operating under wake conditions are more
subjected to surging motions than the one operating in freestream. The reason behind is the
e’¼Ćective ratio between the in’¼éow wind speed and the maximum surging speed V is bigger for
the downstream rotor; note that this will also make the stalling e’¼Ćects of the downstream
rotor stronger.
Is it possible to improve the rotor performances of surging FOWT
by implementing simple controlling strategies?
In chapter 6, it is found that the implemented simple controller can only slightly improve
the power performances of the two rotors to a little degree with moderate V, the power
performances are not improved as signi’¼ücant as the solutions of the quasi-steady states,
which mainly relate to the large rotational inertia I of the system.
7.2
Outlooks and Recommendations
With the cases conducted and analysis made in this thesis project, wake structures,
wake interactions, and rotors performances of surging FOWTs under both laminar and tur-
bulent in’¼éow conditions are systematically overviewed; to the authorŌĆÖs best knowledge, this
comprehensive overview had not been done previously, and this may help other fellow to
quickly grasp the fundamentals of wake structures and wake interactions of surging FOWTs
under both laminar and turbulent in’¼éow conditions.
Page 180 of 244
==PAGE:212==
7.2. Outlooks and Recommendations
By comparing the cases with laminar in’¼éow conditions and turbulent in’¼éow conditions,
it can be found that their wake structures are signi’¼ücantly di’¼Ćerent, especially for the surging
cases. Thus, for the studies related to wake of FOWT in real world, in’¼éow conditions with
realistic TI may be a requirement.
With the analysis carried out with turbulent in’¼éow conditions with realistic turbulent
intensities, the di’¼Ćerences about uDisk between ’¼üxed and surging cases are only about 0.5 ŌćĀ
2%. This indicates the sampling method should be well designed to capture the statistics
accurately and precisely, especially for cases with wake interactions of multiple rotors.
Comparing the cases in chapter 5 and 6, it is found that implementing a simple controller
can help adjust the operational conditions of the surging or ’¼üxed rotors, especially for the
rotors operating under the wake conditions where the in’¼éow velocity is hard to predict. The
controller implemented in this thesis is easy to setup and cost negligible computational e’¼Ćort,
and the parameters for setting the controller should not be di’¼ācult to obtain in most cases.
Results of this thesis show that power performances of surging rotors are highly related
to surging settings, especially for those operating under wake conditions with lower e’¼Ćective
in’¼éow wind speed; thus, having realistic surging settings are important if the goal is to model
systems with realistic FOWT rotors.
For the future works, cases with other degree of freedoms, such as pitching, heaving,
swaying, and rolling (or combinations of multiple degree of freedoms), can be tested to
better understand the wake structures of FOWT, and turbulent in’¼éow conditions should
be considered in order to better simulate real world conditions. Also that cases with more
FOWT rotors can be conducted to understand the wake interactions within the ’¼éoating
o’¼Ćshore wind farms more. Furthermore, this framework may also be used to test newer and
more advanced controlling strategies designed for ’¼éoating o’¼Ćshore wind farms.

Appendix A
Turbulence Noise with Mesh Layouts
As conducting cases of NREL 5MW rotor with laminar in’¼éow conditions, it was found
that the minor turbulence triggered by numerical errors may become signi’¼ücant to the overall
solution to some extent, and they are related to the mesh layouts. Figure A.2 displays the
contour plots of !y (instantaneous y-component of the vorticity vector) on the y = 0 plane
of three cases having identical setups (same as the ’¼üxed case in chapter 4) but with minor
di’¼Ćerences in mesh layouts. For convenience, the three meshes are labeled as con’¼ügurations
1, 2, and 3, and their schematic diagrams can be found in Figure A.1, 3.1, and 3.2. As can
be seen in Figure A.2, the periodic ripples of the vorticities are related to the changing of the
mesh resolutions. However, as observed by the author, this ripples were propagated in up-
stream direction from the rotor at the initial state of the simulations. Moreover, the strength
of these ripples seems to be correlated to the rotor loading distributions. Furthermore, if the
spatial scheme is switched from second order central di’¼Ćerencing (Gauss linear) to second
order upwind di’¼Ćerencing (Gauss linearUpwind grad(U)), the ripples can be much more
alleviated (not shown here). This agrees with many other studies stating that second order
central di’¼Ćerencing would su’¼Ćer bigger numerical oscillations. However, upwind di’¼Ćerenc-
ing is widely considered being over-dissipative, and it will lead to over dissipate vortical
structures in the wakes of wind turbines rotor [61, 98]; thus it is not adopted in this thesis.
(a) yz plane
(b) xz plane
Figure A.1: Schematic diagrams for mesh layouts of mesh con’¼üguration 1. This mesh is an
older attempt during the thesis project.
191
==PAGE:223==
Figure A.2: Contour plots of !y with di’¼Ćerent mesh con’¼ügurations of laminar in’¼éow cases
with NREL 5MW. The dashed-lines indicate the re’¼ünement boundaries in streamwise
direction.
Even though the errors are relatively small (note the scale in Figure A.2), and are al-
most insigni’¼ücant for cases with turbulence in’¼éow conditions, they still cannot be ignored
in laminar cases. Since that even with the very minor ’¼éuctuations, they will trigger the
instability of the wind turbine wake systems and accelerate the break-down process signi’¼ü-
cantly [61]; this can be clearly observed in Figure A.2. Table A.1 and Figure A.3 show more
detail information about the turbulence measured at 2.0D upstream form the rotor with
the three mesh con’¼ügurations, clearly that there are some periodic-like oscillations and the
three con’¼ügurations shared a very similar oscillation frequencies. After comparing, for the
laminar cases, con’¼üguration 3 was selected since it retained the laminar properties the most.
While for the turbulent cases, concept of con’¼üguration 3 was not chosen since the prolonged
distance for turbulent ’¼éow to develop and re’¼ünement regions needed for turbulent inlet will
result in too many cells, also that these ripples are much more diluted with the turbulent
in’¼éow conditions. Considering that con’¼üguration 2 seems able to deliver cleaner laminar
’¼éow than con’¼üguration 1 (one should notice that 2.0D upstream form the rotor falls out
the wake re’¼ünement region for con’¼üguration 1), con’¼üguration 2 is thus chosen as the mesh
layouts for the turbulent cases.

Appendix B
Momentum Entertainment
Term @uu/@x can be written as Equation B.1 by applying chain rule and Reynolds de-
composition (u = u+u
0). And for the wake regions being free from the projected body force
’¼üelds of actuator lines, x-component of the ’¼ültered Navier-Stokes equations Equation 2.7 can
be written as Equation B.2. By taking the time average of Equation B.2, Equation B.3 was
arrived. After implementing Reynolds decomposition, chain-rule, and (’¼ültered) continuity
equation (Equation 2.3), Equation B.3 can be re-written as Equation B.8 through Equa-
tion B.4 to B.7. Plugging Equation B.1 into Equation B.8, Equation B.10 (Equation 2.29)
is arrived.

Appendix C
Sampling and Averaging Methods with
Convergence Test
This chapter describes the sampling and averaging methods. The general strategies for
di’¼Ćerent cases are similar, but the windows considered will vary.
C.1
General Time Scale
In this thesis, TŌī”refer to the time required for NREL 5MW baseline turbine to complete
a revolution with its rated condition, and it is 4.96 s. As for the time step size Ōłåt, it is set
to satisfy Ōī”Ōłåt = 1ŌŚ”, and its absolute value is 0.01378 s.
C.2
Sampling Frequencies for Probes
For the sampling probes to obtain pro’¼üles of u such as Figure 4.3, their sampling rate
is same as Ōłåt, meaning that every timesteps are considered. For every downstream section,
there is 51 probes equally distributed along ŌłÆ0.9 ’Ż┐z/D ’Ż┐0.9, with a spacing of 0.036D.
And the values sampled by this probes are used to calculate uDisk based on weighted averaging
(Equation C.1). Note that a single line was used to represent the complete disk, which would
lead to some inevitable errors.
uDisk =

(C.1)
C.3
Sampling Frequencies for Cutting Planes
For the ’¼üelds data (cutting planes, contours plots) in this thesis, the sampling rate is
15Ōłåt, which is equivalent to sample 24 times for a single rotor revolution under its rated
conditions, and its absolute value is 0.207 s.
200
==PAGE:232==
C.4. Sampling Windows
C.4
Sampling Windows
This section describes the sampling window for the simulation cases, note that these
windows also applies to phase-locked averaging.
C.4.1
Single Rotor with Laminar In’¼éow Conditions
For the single rotor cases with laminar in’¼éow conditions, the total run-time is 80TŌī”for
all cases, and the window between 60TŌī”’Ż┐t ’Ż┐80TŌī”is selected for analyzing statistics, this
corresponds to 5, 10, or 20 complete surging cycles for cases with !S being 0.32, 0.63, or
1.27 rad/s. The relative small window is based on the fact that phase-locked averaged TKE
(<TKE>0ŌćĪ) ’¼üelds are very low for all the cases with laminar in’¼éow conditions, suggesting
the results are not just converged but simply repeating themselves.
C.4.2
Single Rotor with Turbulent In’¼éow Conditions
As for the single rotor cases with turbulent in’¼éow conditions, the sampling windows are
from 60TŌī”’Ż┐t ’Ż┐110TŌī”, corresponds to 13, 25, or 50 complete surging cycles for cases with
!S being 0.32, 0.63, or 1.27 rad/s (for cases with !S = 0.32 rad/s, the sampling window
was extended to 112TŌī”). Note that this means there will be (at least) 1201 samples for the
cutting plane y/D = 0.
C.4.3
Dual Rotors
The sampling windows for cases with dual rotors (without & with controller imple-
mented) are completely identical with cases of single rotor with turbulent in’¼éow conditions.
C.4.4
Exception
The sampling window for obtaining time-averaged ’¼üelds on yz-planes (x-normal planes)
for turbulent cases (eq. Figure 4.48) is 90TŌī”’Ż┐t ’Ż┐110TŌī”, which is smaller than the window
for y = 0 plane.
C.5
Convergence Tests
In order to test whether the interested statistics are converged for the simulation cases,
a brief convergence test with case 41 in Table 5.1 (dual rotors, SS, ŌłåD = 3D, TI = 5.3%)
was carried out. The convergence test was conducted by probing how u, v, w, Žāu, Žāv, &
Žāw, vary with the simulation time (number of samples) at certain positions. The interested
points are at x/D = ŌłÆ2, 3, 6, & 8 with (y, z) = (0.0D, 0.5D) (z = R), and the interested
time window is the one mentioned in subsection C.4.3 (60TŌī”’Ż┐t ’Ż┐110TŌī”, with the length
of t being 50TŌī”). The results of convergence test are shown in Figure C.1 & Figure C.2. It
can be seen that both ’¼ürst order statistics (u, v & w) and second order statistics (Žāu, Žāv,
Page 201 of 244
==PAGE:233==
C.5. Convergence Tests
& Žāw) are well converged. Moreover, in order to test the statistics related to phase-locking
(see Equation 2.22), convergence of < u >0ŌćĪ, < v >0ŌćĪ, < w >0ŌćĪ, < Žāu >0ŌćĪ, < Žāv >0ŌćĪ, &
< Žāw >0ŌćĪare also tested, and they are shown in Figure C.3 & Figure C.4. Note that when
doing analysis with phase-locking data, only 26 samples are available. In general, statistics
related to the phase-locking data also converged, while the convergence of the second order
statistics (< Žāu >0ŌćĪ, < Žāv >0ŌćĪ, & < Žāw >0ŌćĪ) are not as good as Žāu, Žāv, & Žāw.
As for the laminar cases, their solutions are deemed to be converged based on their
very low <TKE>ŽåS ’¼üelds (eg. Figure 4.29 & 5.12), and thus there is no need of conducting
convergence test with them.
