C.6. Remarks
C.6
Remarks
The sampling window for the turbulent cases may not be long enough for for the cases to
be completely converged, especially for the second-order statistics of the phase-locked data.
However, in general, the sampling window are long enough to obtain the desired information,
considering most of the ’¼ürst and second order statistical quantities do converge.
Page 206 of 244
==PAGE:238==
Appendix D
A Brief Proper Orthogonal
Decomposition Analysis
A brief proper orthogonal decomposition analysis is conducted in this chapter. The main
purpose of the analysis is to check if the phase-locked quantities of wakes in this thesis have
any other signi’¼ücant modes other than the phase-locked averaged one (mode 1 is equivalent
to the averaged value). In order to do so, cases with single ’¼üxed & surging rotor under both
laminar and turbulent (TI = 5.3%) in’¼éow conditions (cases 1, 3, 6, & 11) are tested, they
are selected to represent the cases considered in this thesis. In the analysis, !y,0ŌćĪare looked
into, since < !y >0ŌćĪare able to represent the PLSB clearly. Note that only the phase-locked
quantities are looked into, since that the vortical structures in the wakes are repeatable in
time and space, considering the data of all the available timesteps will dilute the distinct
repeatable structures (PLSB).
The POD analysis conducted in this thesis is through the MATLAB function pod, which
is developed by Zigunov [99]. pod wrapped the MATLAB built-in function svd (singular
value decomposition), to make the analysis with series of 2D data (snapshots) easier. The
general concept are shown in Equation D.1 ([U, S, V] = pod(X)), and Einstein notation
is adapted. Subscripts i & j label the coordinates (x & z for the cases here), t indicates the
serial number (timing index) of the inputted 2D snapshots series, k is the index for the mode
shapes, and ╬┤ is Kronecker delta; as for the matrices, X is the series of the 2D snapshots
being analyzed, U is the outputted mode shapes, S is the (root of) energy content for the
outputted mode shapes, and V is the coe’¼ācients for the mode shapes at instant t.
Xijt = UijkSk╬┤kpVpt
(D.1)
Note that the mode shapes number that U has is same as the snapshots number that X
has, and the energy content of k-th mode shapes is SkSp╬┤kp. Note that the outputted mode
shapes U are sequenced base on their energy content. With the sampling windows mentioned
in Appendix C, the laminar cases will have 10 phase-locked samples while the turbulent cases
have 26. In order to better understand the importance of each mode shapes, their energy
content are represented as mode energy fraction, which is described in Equation D.2.
207
==PAGE:239==
D.1. POD with the Surging Cases
Mode Energy Fraction of the k th mode = Sk╬┤kpSp
SqSq
(D.2)
D.1
POD with the Surging Cases
Results of POD analysis of !y,0ŌćĪ(note the vorticity ’¼üelds are non-dimensionalized with
the factor D/V0) with the surging cases (cases 6 & 11) are in Figure D.1. D.2, & D.3, where
the ’¼ürst is the fractions of energy content (Sk), the second is the coe’¼ācients for the mode
shapes at di’¼Ćerent time instants (Vkt), and the third is the mode shapes (Uijk, only the ’¼ürst
four modes are shown). In Figure D.1, it can be seen that mode 1 (averaged-values) for
both laminar and turbulent cases has much more energy content than the others, especially
for the laminar case; moreover, the energy contents of the the turbulent case does not drop
signi’¼ücantly after mode 2. Looking at Figure D.2, it can be seen that except for mode 1,
coe’¼ācients for the other three modes ’¼éuctuate signi’¼ücant, indicating the three modes are
not persist in all of the considered snapshots. While for mode 1, its coe’¼ācients are almost
constant, suggesting the expression of the mode is persist in every snapshots. As viewing the
mode shapes (Figure D.3), mode 1 for both cases are essentially identical to their < !y >0ŌćĪ
’¼üelds (Figure 4.12). While modes 2-4 for the laminar case seems to represent the random
’¼éuctuations after the breakdown of the wake. As for the turbulent case, modes 2-4 seem to
display the released trailing & shedded voticites at the regions just after the rotor, however,
rest of the regions seem to display random ’¼éuctuations related to in’¼éow turbulence as well,
and no periodic features related to !S can be found; also that their timing coe’¼ācients are
not persist as well.

D.3
Remarks
Considering the POD analysis with !y,0ŌćĪ’¼üelds in this chapter, mode 1 (< !y >0ŌćĪ) has
much more importance compare to the other modes. This is based on the facts that the
energy contents of mode 1 are signi’¼ücantly higher than the other modes, and that the timing
coe’¼ācients of mode 1 are almost constant for all modes, where the coe’¼ācients of the other
modes ’¼éuctuate signi’¼ücantly.

E.1
Di’¼Ćerent In’¼éow TI with Single Fixed or Surging Ro-
tor
Figure E.1 and E.2 display the phase-locked averaged and time-averaged vertical velocity
’¼üelds (< w >0ŌćĪ& w) (note the scales are rather small). The blockage e’¼Ćects of the rotor are
clearly shown. Interestingly, for the < w >0ŌćĪ’¼üelds of the surging cases, values of < w >0ŌćĪ
’¼üelds are alternating along the downstream direction signi’¼ücantly even with TI = 5.3%,
which is very unlike to the ’¼üxed rotor cases. This indicates that surging in sinusoidal manner
facilitates the ’¼éow to go into and out from the wake, and they are also related to the vortical
structure discussed in subsection 4.2.3. As for the w ’¼üelds of turbulent cases, the trends are
similar, both showing that the ’¼éows are entrained from outside the wake region for x/D > 1.
The instantaneous and phase-locked averaged pressure ’¼üelds for the eight cases (Ōłåp &
< Ōłåp >0ŌćĪ) are displayed in Figure E.3 and E.4. In the ’¼üelds of Ōłåp for the four turbulent
cases, local minimum and maximum exist, suggesting vortical structures were formed. And
similar with u ’¼üelds, Ōłåp ’¼üelds for surging rotor cases are similar with the ones of ’¼üxed
rotor cases when in’¼éow TI is higher. For the ’¼üelds of < Ōłåp >0ŌćĪfor the turbulent cases
with ’¼üxed rotor, there seems to be some weak local minimums orderly distributed along
the streamwise direction. They might related to the modes of the interactions between tip-
vorticies or Karman vortex street. However, e’¼Ćects of them are not observed in the phase-
locked averaged vorticity ’¼üeld (Figure 4.12), and considering the relatively low magnitudes
scale, they were not further investigated. While for the ’¼üelds of < Ōłåp >0ŌćĪfor the turbulent
cases with surging rotor, periodic structures are clearly observed once again, and the periodic
structures are more visible for the cases with lower TI. Moreover, note that higher and lower
Ōłåp & < Ōłåp >0ŌćĪbubbles are featured mostly at z/D > 0, which are as expected since both
cases all only consider time steps with ŽåŌī”= 0ŌćĪ, as one of the blades (actuator lines) is
pointing upward.
The phase-locked averaged x and z-component of the vorticity (< !x >0ŌćĪ& < !z >0ŌćĪ)
are presented in Figure E.5 and E.6. Fields of < !x >0ŌćĪmainly indicate the wake rotations
214
==PAGE:246==
E.1. Di’¼Ćerent In’¼éow TI with Single Fixed or Surging Rotor
with respect to x-axis, and the distributions of them are as expected (the wakes rotate in
counter-direction of the rotorsŌĆÖ rotations. The direction of the wake rotations were deduced
with StokesŌĆÖ theorem). Fields of < !z >0ŌćĪare presented here for observing shedded vorticies,
which are weak for the ’¼üxed cases; while surging cases possess noticeable values of < !z >0ŌćĪ
just behind the rotor, especially for the laminar cases. Shedded vorticies are an indicator
of whether the lift distributions along the blades (actuator lines) remain close to constant
or not (deduced with Kutta Joukowski theorem together with Kelvin Helmholtz theorem
[100]), and here can see that the lift distributions of the cases with surging rotor vary in
time, while the ones of ’¼üxed cases remain almost constant.

Appendix G
Supplementary Information for Cases
with Simple Controller
In order to better validate the simple torque controller applied in chapter 6 (described in
section 2.7), several additional simulations were carried out. The tested aspects includes the
responding speed of the controller, quasi-steady states performances, e’¼Ćects of the inertia of
the rotor system, behaviours of the controller when subjects to di’¼Ćerent surging frequencies,
and system dynamics when under laminar in’¼éow conditions.
G.1
Inditial Tests
To better understand the e’¼Ćectiveness of the simple torque controller, several additional
simulations about the inditial responds of controller were undertaken. For the setups of
inditial tests, a single ’¼üxed NREL 5MW rotor is placed under the laminar in’¼éow conditions
with di’¼Ćerent in’¼éow velocities V0 (they are chosen based on the interested V0,app in this
thesis, and the idea is same as the cases in Table 4.2), and the controller was activated at
a certain time instant after the system had reached its quasi-steady state; before activating
the controller, the rotor is operating with rotational frequency of Ōī”= Ōī”rated = 1.27 rad/s,
which is the Ōī”for its rated condition. After the activation of the controller, the evolving
histories of CP (both aerodynamic and generator, CP,Aero & CP,Gen) and Ōī”are recorded. As
the system reached its new quasi-steady state, both Ōī”and CP (with reference velocity being
V0,rated = 11.4 m/s) are examined (called them Ōī”

G.2
Inertia Tests
The results in chapter 6 and section G.1 showed that the controller needs a relatively
long periods (compared to the interested surging periods) to respond, and the large rotational
inertia I of the rotor system (rotor plus drive train) is suspected to be the cause of it. To
test the hypothesis, an additional case was ran with smaller rotational inertia, which is 1/50
of the regular rotational inertia.
The test case used the setups of case 83, that is dual
rotors with ŌłåD = 3D, turbulent in’¼éow conditions with TI = 5.3%, and with AS = 4 m
& !S = 0.63 rad/s; rotor performances of the two cases are compared, and the results are
summarized in Table G.2.

G.4. Results Under Laminar In’¼éow Conditions
G.4
Results Under Laminar In’¼éow Conditions
Two additional cases (cases 100 & 101) are presented in this section to display the
system dynamics of controlled rotors under laminar in’¼éow conditions. Case 100 has two
’¼üxed rotors in tandem with ŌłåD = 3D and case 101 has two surging rotors (AS = 4 m &
!S = 0.63 rad/s) in tandem with same ŌłåD, where the both cases are equipped with the same
controller applied in chapter 6. The basic settings and results are summarized in Table G.4.
Figure G.6 to G.8 plots the u, < !y >0ŌćĪ, & <TKE>0ŌćĪ’¼üelds of the two cases together
with other selected cases for comparison, including cases without controller under laminar
in’¼éow conditions (cases 31 & 34) and cases with/without controller under turbulent in’¼éow
conditions (cases 81 & 83 and 38 & 41, TI = 5.3%). Figure G.6 shows that u ’¼üelds are
not signi’¼ücantly altered by the controller (comparing cases 100 & 31 and 101 & 34), and
wake recovery are still absent for the controlled ’¼üxed case under laminar in’¼éow conditions.
Figure G.7 shows that with the controller turned on, the periodic vortical structures (PLSB)
will be more preserved after the downstream rotor for the laminar surging case (case 101)
when comparing to the case without the controller (case 34); this observation comply with
the ’¼ündings in chapter 6, showing that turbulent cases with the controller turned on have
sharper PLSB than the ones without the controller. Figure G.8 shows that the values of
<TKE>0ŌćĪ’¼üelds for the laminar cases with the controller are still quite low, but not as low as
the cases without the controller, especially at the regions vicinity to the rotors as well as the
paths of the convected tip/root voticies. It is as expected that the values of <TKE>0ŌćĪ’¼üelds
very close to the rotors for the laminar cases with controller are higher than those without
controller, since with the controller introduced, Ōī”will not be guaranteed to be exactly a
constant, and the phase-locked relationship between Ōī”and !S may not be maintained.
